B008MG1E4A|1.0|Disclaimer first: I am a veteran of the U.S. Army. I served in Afghanistan during '09-'10 as a combat medic in support of Operation Enduring Freedom.<br /><br />This book is a classic example of just what a great agent/publicity department can do for a wholly unremarkable work if it catches the world at just the right time. For those looking for the cliff's notes, here goes: If you've read/seen the \\"leaked\\" blurbs of this book on the news, then you've got all the \\"juicy\\" secrets this book has to offer. There's no bomb shells here and the writing is at times discursive. After reading this book one thing is patently obvious: Matt is a man with a COLOSSAL ego. This book is all about Matt: Matt's heroism; Matt's struggles; Matt's accomplishments; Matt's great shooting; and ultimately what appears to be Matt's grudge against Team 6. As a former soldier to other soldiers I would say this is a book \\"all about me instead of we\\" from a guy who clearly feels that way. Does Matt reveal details that will jeopardize future operations? In my unprofessional opinion, unlikely. But is Matt a Blue Falcon (military for Buddy f'er)? You betcha. Now to my review.<br /><br />Matt appears to be a guy with an axe to grind who ran head long into a historical event, and a corporate publishing house that knows how to turn a fast buck. Matt Bissonnette, aka Mark Owen, doesn't like President Obama. Mr. Bissonnette likely parted SEAL Team 6 on terms less than ideal by his thinking. This book is his attempt to run Team 6 through the mud in such a way as to make it impossible for them to respond without disclosing their identities. Obama is a politician after Machiavelli's heart, and Joe Biden reminds them of someone's \\"drunk uncle\\": so says Team 6, according to Mr. Bissonnette.<br />(Begin quote from book)<br />\\"You know we just put admiral's stars on Jay,\\" Walt said during the speech [President Obama informing the nation of the successful raid]. \\"And we just got this guy reelected.\\"<br />\\"Well,would you rather not have done this?\\" I said.<br />\\"We all knew the deal.\\"<br />[Here Matt decides to speak for all of Team 6; and apparently the entire SOCOM community as well]<br />\\"We were tools in their toolbox, and when things go well they promote it. They inflate their roles.\\" (end quote).<br />Such a quote serves only one purpose, and that purpose is not to \\"set the record straight\\" or \\"honor those who made it possible.\\" It's a kneecap job. Plain and simple. Just to make his intentions perfectly clear, Mr. Bissonnette delivers a coup de grace. [Obama meets with Team 6 to congratulate them. Biden is there, evidently, and makes \\"lame jokes.\\" Team 6, according to Matt, is not impressed.]<br />(Begin quote)<br />\\"I don't recall much about the speech. It was straight from the speechwriter playbook:<br />'You guys are America's best.' [Obama speaking] 'You are what America stands for.' 'Thank you from the American people.' 'Job well done.'\\"<br />(End quote)<br />After the speech Obama purportedly invites Team 6 to the White House for a beer. Walt, a fellow member of Team 6, just \\"smirks.\\" Sometime later Matt realizes that the \\"invite\\" hasn't come and says to Walt.<br />(Begin quote)<br />\\"Hey, did you ever hear anything about that beer,\\" I asked.<br />Walt's smirk was back.<br />\\"You believed that sh@!,\\" [profanity altered], he said. \\"I bet you voted for change too, sucker.\\"<br />(end quote).<br />Two things: One, as a former soldier, what is said in uniform, stays in uniform. Two, \\"outing\\" the supposed political opinions of men who cannot respond is beyond the pale, and beneath a former SEAL. That's not setting the record straight. That's trying to ruin others reputations. Sounds like there's bad blood between Matt and Team 6. And really, there's nothing more to the book. I shelled out 13 bucks for it. As a former soldier and a veteran of Afghanistan, I would have bought this book no matter what the reviews were. My recommendation to those out there is to take a pass. Really, it's poorly written and there's nothing groundbreaking here AT ALL. Just a pissed off former SEAL looking for some cheap payback.<br /><br />Edit: So here's what I think are two fair and well thought out points brought up by comments on this review.<br />1. Are you an Obama supporter? Is that your beef?<br />2. What's the big deal with Matt talking about soldier's supposed political view points? Don't they have the right to speak their minds. And Matt is out of uniform, so why can't he talk about other soldiers?<br />I'll take question two first because it will answer #1 in my opinion.<br />Do soldiers have the right to speak their minds while in uniform? I'll try to keep my answer as brief as possible. First the one word reply, NO. Now the reason. Suppose the military decided tomorrow that Obama shouldn't be president. How would We The People go about stopping them? Do you think a rifle in the hands of a civilian is a match for a main battle tank commanded by a trained soldier? What stops the men and women in uniform from overthrowing our civilian government isn't a fear of the Second Amendment, it's a sense of honor and duty. Maybe that sounds corny to you, but that's the gist of it. There are millions of people, right now, in this world living under a military junta. Our military is hands down the strongest in the world. We The People are no match for it.<br />Now I'll expand on that idea. Suppose Obama wins the upcoming election. To be frank, I hope he loses. But suppose he wins. Suppose another mission comes up with another high value target. That's not far fetched at all. What if Obama is sitting in the White House and being briefed on the assets available? Delta Force says they can do the job; the Navy says its SEALs are up to the task. What if Obama picks Delta Force? What if, only for a tenth of a second, Obama thinks \\"Would the SEALs burn me down?\\" Is that something we want any president to think? Do you want the president worried that the military plays favorites? Now suppose Obama rises above it and picks Delta simply because they are best for the job. What do you think the admiral in charge of the SEALs that just lost the mission would think? \\"This is because my SEALs can't keep their mouths shut. Obama didn't pick my guys because my guys said they hate Obama.\\"<br />These two ideas: that the military must never dominate the civilian government; and that the politicians must never come to think the military plays favorites, or would act to damage a politician, are well understood and vital to our democracy. Ask Gen McChrystal and Gen MacArthur and Gen Fuller what happens when a soldier gets near that line. So what's my point? Matt knows this. Every soldier knows this. Ask yourself this: What is something like, \\"I bet you voted for change too, sucker,\\" doing in a book that's supposed to be about the Osama mission? To me the answer is clear. Matt used America's interest in hearing anything new about the mission (of which there's nothing here) as cover to tag his old teammates as Obama haters. That tag will stick with them and the SEAL community for a while. It will damage their careers. In my opinion, that was the ONLY reason this book was written. He could have waited until after the election. That's just two months away. Why publish now? This book does maximum damage in a close election by making it look like the military is bias.

B001DF68MA|4.0|Carrying your baby in the front of the bike where you can see them and talk to them is much better than the back. They can get into all kinds of trouble back there. Especially having less to look at, they'll get bored. Babies love to interact with people you pass as you ride, and they can look up and see your face. If there's a problem with the straps or anything, you can see it and fix it.<br /><br />You have to be aware that they can mess with your brakes or push your handlebar with their hands and feet, so take the time to get used to it before doing anything ambitious. Even under the best conditions, your attention will always be divided between riding your bike and what your kid is doing, so you have to think about the inherent risk.<br /><br />Having spent time with both, I prefer the WeeRide LTD to a seat that mounts on the handlebars. It's more stable, especially when on the kickstand. You still can't walk away from the bike with the kid in the seat, but at least the kickstand will hold it up. I recommend using this in combination with a Click-Stand folding bike stand.<br /><br />The sleeping pad works great. The main reason I wanted to try this seat was that the iBert Safe-T-Seat I had didn't support the baby when she went to sleep, so she just slumped over. Apparently the newer iBerts have this feature.<br /><br />With a seat that pushes their legs right up under your handlebar, it's also a problem to stuff the baby's feet under the handlebars in seats like the iBert, and even more of a problem to get the kid out of the seat when you stop. You wish you had three hands. Only a high-rise handlebar avoids this problem with the iBert.<br /><br />The massive steel rail of the WeeRide that mounts to the steering head and the seat tube is way too heavy, but it's very simply made and probably can be replaced with some aluminum stock. Keep in mind that you need some clearance to attach this rail above your top tube. If your head tube is flush, the WeeRide won't work at all. The head tube should stick up above the top tube at least 1/4\\" to just squeak by; I'd look for more like 3/8\\" to be certain. Go to WeeRide's web site and download the PDF instructions and read them so you know how this is supposed to work before you buy.<br /><br />The straps and the buckle on the LTD are better than some, but like most car seats and other baby seat belts, they're a pain. I use a Bongo Tie as a chest strap so seat's straps don't fall down off their shoulders.<br /><br />The other trade off with this type of child seat is that it fills up the space between your handlebars and saddle, so you probably won't be able to stand over the top tube. You have to lower your seat post a bit and stand on your toes while on your saddle, unless your top tube is extra long and you and adjust your saddle very far back. In addition, because the child seat is near your knees, you will probably have to spread your knees a bit wide as you pedal. It works, but if you tried to ride fifty miles that way it might be really uncomfortable. This seat is appropriate for slow rides of only a few miles.<br /><br />The bottom line is, will you and your kid want to use this -- or any -- bike seat? It depends on you and your kid. Borrow a seat if you can and see if it's something you're into before spending any money.k at all. I put love in quotes because the story is really about infatuation and not what I would consider love. I really think a better story would have involved characters who were a little bit older, with more life and sexual experience, a male character with more balanced personality, a female character with...well, a personality and a female character who actually enjoyed the new experiences because she was a good match for him. I don't think such sophisticated, young and somewhat wealthy and well raised girls should swear this much if not at all!<br /><br />- Not to mention, the girls smoke! It's just not right... I'm not against women smoking, but I think 16year olds shouldn't. (Again, a personal opinion.).<br /><br />The characters:<br /><br />Alison DiLaurentis (a.k.a Ali): She's somewhat the main character in this book. A very malicious, deadly and unfriendly girl who becomes friends with Aria, Spencer, Emily and Hanna. Only she's mostly toying with them, taken their secrets and then threating to expose them if they don't do what she says. She disappears in the summer of their seventh grade and doesn't show up. The girls thought they're safe because their secrets are gone. Guess what? They were wrong.<br /><br />Aria Montgomery: The hippy friend. She's been been always made fun of by Ali for her weird style of dressing. After Ali's disappearance, she stayed a year and a half in Iceland and came back later for her junior year of High School. She meets a very attractive guy and they fall in love instantly. Later on, turns out that this guy is her AP English teacher, Mr. Ezra Fitz. Throughout the book, Aria goes into a lot of trouble keeping an old secret and a new one, but unfortunately, A gets to her and start warning her. Aria is very nice, friendly and cute in her own way. She's one of my favorite characters in this book :).<br /><br />Emily Fields (a.k.a Em or Emy): The jock girl. A very nice, shy and good swimmer. She's the shiest in the group and tries to keep to herself. She tries so hard to hide the fact that she's gay from everyone thinking that they'll judge her and hate her. However, when she meets Maya, the new girl living in Ali's house, she starts to have very serious second thoughts about herself and her feelings. \\"A\\" obviously doesn't miss a chance to torture her and ruin her life.<br /><br />Hanna Marin (a.k.a Han): Han has serious problems and very bad morals (part of why I don't like her this much in these books so far - reading Flawless-). She used to be treated very badly by Alison, who used to call her Hefty Hanna, because she was fat and had grease everywhere in her body. When Ali disappeared, Hanna continued her commitment to try harder and be the next It Girl, which she achieved, using every thing she could do or get. Now the most popular girl in Rosewood Day, she faces other issues, including \\"A\\". She's now dating her longtime crush Sean Ackard who happens to be a member of the Virginity Club. Her father also comes back in her life and things are complicated again. \\"A\\", as always, being the \\"nice\\" person, intervenes only to make things even worse.<br /><br />Spencer Hastings (a.k.a Spence): The smartest in the group. Her family is very strict, serious and always the first in everything. She has a very nice and unique personality (she's my 2nd favorite character in this book :).) with loopholes as usual. She's always the one who stood up to Ali and was the last one who saw her before she went missing. Now, three years later, she's as determined and serious as ever, wanting to beat her older sister's, Melissa's, records. But, there's one thing she always wants despite her having everything; her sister's boyfriends. And so, she meets her sister's soon to be fianc&eacute; Wren who's British, an intern to become a doctor, funny and cute. And the problems begin and get more complicated as \\"A\\" threatens to share her secrets, including those only her and Ali knew about.<br /><br />Other characters worth mentioning: Mona, Hanna's best friend since Ali's disappearance. She used to be harassed by Ali and her group (although Aria, Em, Han and Spence never said anything to help or stop Ali) for being a nerd and she tried so hard to be Ali's friend, but never succeeded, until Ali wen missing. Mike, Aria's younger brother. Darren Wilden, a cop who almost arrests Hanna. Ashley, Hanna's mother. Byron and Ella, Aria's parents.<br /><br />Favorite quotes:<br /><br />You wonder how someone so...well, so much like you went missing. You thought only girls who entered beauty pageants ended up on the sides of milk cartons.<br /><br />Well, think again. --- How it All Started.<br /><br />Aria headed into the classroom and fell into the first desk she saw. She put her head down and took heaving, emotional breaths.<br /><br />\\"Hell is other people,\\" she chanted. 0441020712|5.0|A novel which strives to impart the 'Overview Effect' upon it's reader, that shift an astronaut experiences when the focus of his world view is shifted forever.  Blue Remembered Earth, is one of Reynolds best works and it is a difficult task which Reynolds took upon himself in trying to impart that cognitive shift upon the reader, and as such it will not be successful for every reader. There are several negative reviews of this book, in which I feel, the reviewer failed to make the leap.<br /><br />The book starts as a standard quest narrative, and focuses the reader in on tiny details as one tries to put together the pieces like a mystery novel. All the while a huge plot is constructed around the plot thread. When the final reveal occurs the whole focus changes outward to a very large field of focus. This book *is* space euphoria if you can make that leap from written fiction. This elevation of perspective is the goal of good science fiction, and this work achieves that goal. A euphoric read for serious science fiction fans.

0375842209|5.0|The Book Thief is set during World War II in the town of Molching, just outside of Munich. It is about a girl named Liesel Meminger, who is the book thief. She first steals a book at her brother's funeral, and then is brought to Molching to live with Hans and Rosa Hubermann, her foster parents. Hans is very kind, and he teaches her how to read, and soon she is stealing books from Nazi book burnings and the mayor's wife library, among other places. Then Max Vanderberg, a Jew, arrives, and is sheltered in their basement, changing Liesel. All very well, you may say, but the interesting thing about this one is that the book is narrated by...Death. Death himself. I thought that was certainly an interesting idea, especially in a time of war, when  Death is so busy. The interludes where Death muses on...well, death, for the most part, aren't quite as clever as Zusak seems to think they are, though are intriguing, and foreshadow what's to come. And amusing, somehow, with a sort of grim, dark, humor. I enjoyed The Book Thief just as much as before; there's just something about it that makes it very compelling indeed. The Book Thief is certainly not an easy book to read, to grapple with, and it can be very sad and also moving. Zusak's style in this one is also very distinctive, quite different from his other book, I Am the Messenger. I would recommend to everyone.<br /><br />All of my reviews can be read at my blog novareviews.blogspot.com.

B0000067L4|5.0|Simply one of the greatest rock albums ever released. Or, if you like, three of the greatest live tracks ever released with 3 studio tracks added on but not an 'album'. What does it matter? Because the only thing wrong with this CD is that it doesn't contain a fourth live track instead of, or in addition to, the three short studio tracks.<br />With CD allowing much more space than the original LP it's a disgrace that Polydor haven't rectified this. With one or two extra live tracks it would have the makings of what discophiles like to think of as an 'album' and people compiling recommended discographies might stop moaning about it being a(n)(arbitrary) hodgepodge and completely forgetting to rate it primarily on the musical quality of the individual tracks rather than as some sort of meaningful, tidy or satisfying arrangement of numbers.<br />This 'record' has been sadly undervalued ever since its release simply because it is neither a studio album nor a live album although there was an obvious reason for this since there were 3 studio tracks left over after Cream broke up. The three very well chosen live numbers which were added used up all the remaining space on the vinyl format. What more could people want?<br /><br />But a friendly warning, this is not for the fainthearted, or the musically fainthearted. These live tracks need to be given total concentrated attention, the sort of attention you would give to a Beethoven development section. Nevertheless, music of this split-brain intensity in the playing, and Nietzschean heroism in the vocals, is not for everyone, and I fear there's nothing to be done about that. But I suppose there is always the much tamer 'Wheels of Fire' studio album which has two of these numbers, 'Politician' and 'Sitting On Top of The World', for those who want something less demanding - primarily less demanding in terms of passion; the concentrated contrapuntal tension is what is necessary to produce it. But when Bruce is on good vocal form, as he is here, his singing is immeasurably more passionate than anything he or anyone else, except perhaps Plant, could achieve in the studio.<br /><br />For those who do appreciate this album there is unfortunately only one other disc to match it. That is the live 'Wheels Of Fire' disc with the very famous 'Crossroads' and the 16min live version of 'Spoonful'. I think this live 'Spoonful' is probably the finest, noblest achievement of rock music so far, both on account of Bruce's vocal performance and the superbly maintained tension and architecture of the long middle section. I have heard quite a few of their live Spoonfuls on bootlegs etc. but this one is undoubtedly the best. The three live tracks on 'Goodbye' run it pretty close and are the next most important pieces we have.<br />However, it is also worth knowing that there are at least 2 very fine live performances on 'Live Cream Vol.2' ( Sunshine Of Your Love and Politician) and that there is a longer and better version of N.S.U. to be found (only)as part of the 4disc set 'Those Were The Days' which also contains  better stereo balanced mixes of the less satisfactory ones on both 'Live Cream Vols.1 and 2'. It's also worth noting Bruce's particularly fine vocal on 'Deserted Cities Of The Heart' on LC Vol.2. - another magnificent example of what can only be found in Cream as I have described above.<br />But the stereo mixes of these three tracks are as good as they can be, and 'I'm So Glad' in particular is far better than the mix in the box-set. The other mix of 'Glad' is only useful if you want to study exactly what Clapton is playing since the lead guitar is brought much further forward. Unfortunately this destroys the competitive balance with the bass and the performance ends up being much less exciting than it is on this original album<br /><br />It's obvious that what the record company should have done long ago (as soon as Cream started appearing om CD) is to move the studio tracks on this album to the Wheels Of Fire studio album where they really belong and would work well as fillers to bulk it up a bit. The remaining live tracks should have been supplemented with various other live tracks which have emerged since the original vinyl albums - there is at least one bootleg in fine quality of an Oakland concert which has different versions of Spoonful, Crossroads, Sunshine and others, and there is another Sunshine released on a Clapton CD single as a third track as well as the NSU mentioned above in the box set. There are also different performances of Crossroads, Sunshine and Spoonful on wolfgangsvault.

B0000067L3|5.0|This performance of 'Spoonful' is the '9th Symphony'* of Rock, combined with 'The Rite Of Spring'. Jack Bruce is the human race individualised. He is the first and last man on earth. He is Job, Prometheus, Mani and Zarathustra. His song is a great humanist assertion or a great song of Gnostic defiance or both. You say it's just a blues with two chords? No, it's the blues transcended. It's the apotheosis of the blues - and it could only be achieved with Bruce's heroic jouissance as a singer, and simultaneously playing bass which is partly where the incredible tension comes from: i.e doing both: Robert Johnson achieved a similar contrapuntal tension singing and playing the guitar at the same time but only connoiseurs seem to be able to hear it.<br />No one else could have achieved this, and the performance of course also depends and benefits from the tensions contributed by the other two as well. These tensions, need I say, are mainly rhythmic and contrapuntal (rhythmic contradictions and ambiguities are also what this neo-contrapuntalism is all about). And that's all there even before you get to the improvisation which is sustained three part contrapuntal concentration but with plenty of light and shade and variation of dynamics. The most powerful example in music of the neo-contrapuntal style apart from this is Stravinsky's 'Rite Of Spring'. There aren't really any other examples worth mentioning of it being the sustained principle of the work, or the piece, outside of a handfull of other live Cream performances. Otherwise there are only a few examples, usually much shorter and less rigorous, of live rock music by such groups as Led Zeppelin.<br />Cream did occasionally play straight blues but in their best work they achieved a Beethovenian affirmation of life in the face of everything that ALL human beings are confronted with. In these performances the blues has come a long way from social marginalisation and right to the existential centre. In other words there isno longer any sense of this being the music of the powerless underdog or self-conscious social deviant.<br />Many other people have said a lot about Crossroads and given it very high praise which is fine by me except inasmuch as they neglect Spoonful (and 'I'm So Glad', 'Politician', and 'Sitting On Top Of The World' in the live versions found on 'Goodbye Cream').<br /><br />The studio disc here is disappointing, as ever with Cream, but at least this time the stereo sound stage is normal and the sound and placing of the drums is convincing. The problem is with the overdubbing of guitars and the usual studio frigidity of the whole thing. Another problem is the thin, brittle sound of Clapton's guitars. The remastered versions on the 4disc set 'Those Were The Days' are better in this respect with a warmer guitar sound.<br /><br />*Beethoven's obviously.

Am I glad I did. Once the two protagonists meet and you start to hear the life story of the elderly protagonist there is no stopping.<br /><br />\\"I will tell the story my way, in order. No questions, no jumping around,\\" warns Vida Winter, the beloved author whose life story is being recorded for the first time. And tell it she does. Of course, she's never told the truth before when it comes to her story so can we be sure what we're hearing now is the truth, the whole truth?<br /><br />Corroborating stories lend a Roshoman-esque quality at times but it is the growing relationship between the author and her biographer that really sets the scene. The story grows steadily as more color and texture is added and just when you think you know the whole story a new thread is introduced and the pattern is enhanced and becomes even more vibrant. This is a story I was sorry to see end.<br /><br />Do you believe in ghosts?

B000SEGUDE|5.0|I love reading books that are refreshingly unique and surprising, and Cloud Atlas is definitely one of them.  Cloud Atlas consists of six chapters set in six different and ascending time periods, with the first five ending halfway through in cliffhangers.  Each story involves a character discovering the previous chapter/time period's story in various forms, and just like the reader, each main character finds only half of the previous story.  But have no fear, the sixth chapter is told in completion and then the next five revisit the remaining five stories in reverse order, picking up the cliffhangers and carrying them to the end.<br /><br />After a strong recommendation from a close friend with similar reading tastes, I gave Cloud Atlas a try and was initially very turned off by it.  Mitchell gives each chapter a distinct voice, which often means frustrating dialects, and many chapters are written as diary entries or letters, which often means cryptic references or shorthands.  Thanks to the encouragement of my friend I pushed through and found that while I may not always like the main characters or the style of writing in a particular chapter, I loved the stories and layered themes.  I was also impressed that Mitchell could write these chapters/short stories in such difference voices and styles and yet snake through them continuations, linking them all together.  There are a lot of themes running through these chapters, especially about human nature and cyclical patterns, but for the sake of avoiding any spoilers, I won't go into any further detail except to point out my least and favorite chapters.  I won't include any real spoilers, but if you don't want to know anything about the individual stories and want to be surprised when you start each chapter then don't read any further...My least favorite chapter was probably the Cavendish one - I just couldn't get invested in the character and while the story was entertaining it seemed almost like filler to me.  My favorite chapter was the Sonmi one, set in a futuristic South Korea filled with clones that are created to perform the more menial tasks.  Sonmi's world was not only fascinating, but her story was exciting, dark, deep, and nail bitingly tense at times.  I also found her to be one of the most interesting and complex main characters.<br /><br />Each chapter definitely has its highs and lows, but taken as a whole Cloud Atlas is an amazing read.  I couldn't put it down and would've finished it faster if only I didn't have the stupid inconvenience of going to work.  I highly recommend you give this book a try, and please give it a fair shot at grabbing your interest because that first chapter can be a bit of a turn-off at first.  But if you're in the mood for a good read (not some sort of light and fluffy brain candy, but a more complex and interesting read), then start with chapter one of Cloud Atlas and don't stop until you hit the end of chapter six.  If you still don't like it and don't care how each of the stories will end then sure put it aside and move on to another book. 0525953728|4.0|I have to split this review in two parts. The first is the content of the book, and the second is if it should have even been written.<br /><br />First off the story. The very nature of the elite teams of the US military, the Navy SEALS (in particular their Team Six) and the Army Delta Force,  have lived in a black ops world where the idea is you preform in service of country, you seek no recognition for, and you simply don't talk about it. Over the decades the SEALs have lowered these standards (some, but far less books on Delta Force have been released) and over the last decade literally dozens of books have been released detailing their lives, many of them bestsellers. This book has really pushed the farthest revealing blow by blow much of how the biggest raids, like the Osama raid, are executed. While the author makes pains to not glorify himself in particular, it strikes as disingenuous his attacks on senior Officers and President Obama that they inappropriately were leaking details of their operations for Political gain (which I agree with) but failing to see that it is easy to construe the very authorship of the book a year after the raid as the same.<br /><br />In a way I wish these secrets remained secrets, but they are out there, and this book is a beautifully written account of a hidden world of men who protect our nation daily while we go about our lives, enjoying the freedom they provide.

I wish I had a chance to read it years ago. I've struggled with my concept of what a developer should be when compared to the state of software development today. At times I would feel like my dedication to technical excellence was at odds with what matters to business. This book helped me realize that not only was I right to try to raise the bar but that I was still setting the bar too low.<br /><br />This books is just a beginning in that it paves the road to becoming a professional developer. It contains a number of concepts that should be studied and applied but it also urges you to continue the journey beyond what is covered in the book.<br /><br />Some would argue that he sets the bar too high and that this will cause frustration for developers. Most true developers want to be better than they are but often struggle with the culture that derides that type of commitment.<br /><br />I remember scoffing at those developers who would discuss design patterns thinking that they were not interested in real work. I mocked Test Driven Development as a fad and a waste of time. It was only after years of struggling with unmanageable code did I realize that something was fundamentally wrong with how code usually got written. (2) The stock market is open, well-regulated, and can be participated in by most of us; the bond market is closed, essentially unregulated, deceptive, and closed to all but the wealthy, corporations and investment banks. (3) Shorting stocks carries unlimited risk. But shorting bonds using the deceptively-named credit default swaps carries very little risk that remains fixed. (4) The bond rating agencies were both duped and part of the game; they are essentially worthless. (5) Extreme avarice and simple unchecked greed underlie it all. B005LAIH3G|5.0|If you are in the mood for a fun sci-fi fantasy adventure, then look no farther.  JOHN CARTER is your movie.  This movie sucked me right in and put me in a world that I didn't just want to be in but fight for.  While it was in theatrical release, I saw it nine times.  That's NINE times.  I hadn't seen a film that many times in a cinema since the original STAR WARS debuted.  As other reviews point out, you may have seen some of the elements of John Carter in Star Wars, Avatar, Superman or  Flash Gordon, but that's because all these properties \\"borrowed\\" extensively from the John Carter novels published 100 years ago.  Some critics berated the film for showing these elements anyway, a line in the sand arbitrarily drawn by them just for this movie.  Like the original Star Wars movie and the Indiana Jones films, John Carter has a Saturday matinee appeal to it and the 19th-century setting adds a certain time-lost charm to it.  And the plot is refreshingly deeper than you might expect from a movie of this sort.  If you have kids, they will absolutely love Woola and you probably will to.  The two lead actors, Taylor Kitsch and Lynn Collins, are virtual unknowns but I believe that works in the story's favor as you're figuring out their characters.  In any case, they're both likeable and athletic enough and Ms. Collins does an admirable job of turning what could easily be merely a 2-D pinup and turning her into a flesh-and-blood person.  Willem Dafoe, Samantha Morton, Mark Strong, James Purefoy, Ciaran Hinds and the rest of the cast all turn in enjoyable performances.  Bryan Cranston from \\"Breaking Bad\\" even has a small role in the movie.  The only relevant complaint I heard about this movie was after one of the showings was from two kids complaining it ran a little long (it's 2 hours, 12 minutes), an observation that applies to the Pirates of the Caribbean films as well.  That said, the first few viewings that I saw had people in the audience clapping at the end, myself included.<br /><br />Now the other reality.  While John Carter did well overseas (it set records in Russia), it did not do great business here at home.  This is not the fault of the film.  Disney did a very poor job of marketing it.  You have to soften the ground a bit when you bring back a 100-year-old franchise that the mainstream public no longer remembers.  You have to re-introduce it, give them the history, remind them why the property is important and ground-breaking.  But no, Disney Studios treated John Carter like it was just another action film.  Disney did not show respect to the property and the American public followed their lead.  At the same time, a decision seems to have been made by the Hollywood media to take Disney to task for funding expensive tentpole movies like Pirates of the Caribbean, John Carter, etc. (which prevents small productions from getting made) so the fix was in to bury John Carter before it even debuted.<br /><br />But you probably don't need to know all that.  All you need to know is this: If swashbuckling adventure with a strong, brave hero and a devastatingly beautiful, kick-ass princess both fighting for a worthy cause is your thing,  JOHN CARTER is definitely your movie.  This movie is at least worth a rental but I HIGHLY recommend you purchase it.  Because if you're like me, you're going to want to own it.<br /><br />A possible sequel is still in doubt and could use your help.  If you're like me, you're going to want to go back to Barsoom.  There's a petition available online.  Just do a search for john carter sequel petition after you see the movie.  Kaor!

0374203032|2.0|I will not comment on his philosophy in the area of Politics and Justice, but the area of economics must be a complete mystery to the good professor and deserves a response.<br />Below are a few quotations from  Sandel's book.  They are a small sample, but a good indication of his embrace of virulent progressivism as a philosophy regarding the political economy.<br /><br />Page 7  \\"...It was the expansion of markets, and market values, into spheres of life where they don't belong.\\"<br /><br />Page 10  \\"These examples illustrate a broader point:  some of the good things in life are corrupted or degraded if turned into commodities.  So to decide where the market belongs, and where it should be kept at a distance, we have to decide how to value the goods in question - health, education, family life, nature, art, civic duties, and so on.  These are moral and political questions, not economic ones.  To resolve them, we have to debate, case by case, the moral meaning of these goods and the proper way of valuing them.\\"<br /><br />Page 11  \\"The great missing debate in contemporary politics is about the role and reach of markets.  Do we want a market economy, or a market society?  What role should markets play in public life and personal relations?  How can we decide which goods should be bought and sold, and which should be governed by nonmarket values?  Where should money's writ not run?\\"<br /><br />Page 88 has this gem:  \\"The notion that economics is a value-free science independent of moral and political philosophy has always been questionable.  But the vaunting ambition of economics today makes this claim especially difficult to defend.  The more markets extend their reach into noneconomic spheres of life, the more entangled they become with moral questions.\\"<br /><br />Many such phrases come early as in the introduction, throughout the book, and cast doubt on Sandel's understanding of the market.  He appears not to know that the market economy is simply the total of all trades individuals make with each other.  I am not sure he understands that every value within the law has an economic base.  He apparently feels the market is a living thing that can influence the tides of the political economy, that somehow markets direct human activity and are to be controlled.<br /><br />But the market really consists only of numbers.  Those numbers occur when people freely decide to trade or not trade.  Individuals exchanging goods and services are the market and use a myriad of thought processes of which we know nothing.  Nor do we need to know why people trade or not trade because they will likely change those thought processes in an unknown manner for the next trade or refusal to trade.  The morals or even the justice of the trade is of no interest to the economist.  He only cares that no coerced action has occurred and the price is in evidence.  Economists like to follow trends to predict the future because it is all they have until they are willing to accept a workable theory of money and credit.  Trends only work until the unpredictable actors who make up the market change their minds.  It is true that no economic crash has ever been predicted by a consensus in economists.  They will always miss it because they cannot know the collective individuals thought processes from which the market acts.  They can see the past trends, but are completely ignorant of rapid future changes.  The only things Sandel can control in the market are the people.  He can only do that by resorting to violence or threat of violence, and enlisting the aid of government, which has the monopoly on violence.<br /><br />The tenor of his book leads me to believe Sandel is trying to apply epistemology to economic activity.  These are two different fields.  I know that I am way over my head when I try to relate human thinking to the mechanics of action and vice-versa.<br /><br />Dr. Sandel leaves no doubt he blames our economic problems on the market.  He tells us themarket is faulty.  He is completely wrong, as are most of his far left colleagues who float from Universities to think tanks to government service and back.  They blame the \\"failing market\\" for the results of their own massive interference with the market.  More than enough written economic knowledge and history is available to blow that progressive theses out of the water.  We can look back at about a dozen major economic disruptions since the founding of our country.  Each one, whether it is called Contraction, Panic, Depression, or Recession, has been caused by rapidly expanding the supply of money and credit from thin air.  Lower interest rates from these counterfeiting operations always eventually lead to enough misallocation of capital to lower the demand for the goods people really want below the point at which those loans, with interest, can be repaid.  The market does not fail, but the government does.<br /><br />After a time of large credit expansion, the economy always crashes.  This is happening now.  Over the last several decades, our twelve trillion dollar economy has managed to load itself up with over fifty trillion dollars in non-performing loans - that is, loans made for projects unable to generate enough growth to service that debt.  The money for such vast borrowing never did exist, having been created from nothing.  Our treasury now is foolish enough to attempt relief by dropping interest rates to near zero and then trying to paper over the problem by creating more credit from nothing.  Would you fix a problem by doubling down on the process that caused the problem?  It never works, but somehow Sandel wants to blame the whole thing on that nasty market.<br /><br />Left to its own devices, the market is self-adjusting.  Whenever political interference with the market warps it far enough, those that made the wrong bets must liquidate the losses and someone else takes over their assets.  All the actors in the market then move forward with appropriate exchanges consistent with productivity and the market again gives accurate statistical data in the best manner to facilitate full use of the market prices.  Growth returns.  Dr Sandel and his cohort either do not understand or refuse to acknowledge the fact that the political act of printing money and credit to bail out the those who acted on false market data and lost is, in fact, only adding more damage to the economy on top of the original damage, thus deepening and further delaying the correction.<br /><br />Dr. Sandel uses many examples, including queues, to illustrate his points.  Unfortunately, he applies economics to solve perceived problems outside the realm of economics.  Usually he ends up in the fields of social justice and politics.  For instance, take his analysis of what he calls Lexus Lanes.  These are our familiar HOV (High Occupancy Vehicle) automobile traffic lanes arbitrarily assigning priority to cars containing more than one person.  I do not think he even understands that economic analysis does not apply to the situation.  No market forces were evident in bringing HOV lanes into existence.  It was all done by government decree and a modicum of bribery for the cities who really had no choice in the matter, let alone those individuals who make the market.  These lanes would never have been developed within the market because the cost of the lanes is so high due to additions to present roads and control features that their cost approaches that of a properly designed highway with enough free lanes to handle the traffic.  Dr. Sandel falls into the trap of automatically assigning full political control to situations best left to the market.  Progressives, such as the author firmly believe that government leaders know far more than the masses and that the proper duty of government is to take control of individual's lives - a most unnatural state of affairs, that has the opposite effect of bettering the masses.<br /><br />Throughout the book, Dr.Sandel uses the word, \\"We\\" in reference to deciding how to determine what goods and services must be bought and sold and what goods and services will not be sold.  Obviously he believes government should be the \\"We\\" and since the market, by definition, is only made of interpersonal exchange, then \\"We\\", the actors, must be subjected to coercion, which means the market is no longer free.  Which also means totalitarian political pressure abrogates the market.<br /><br />Of course, markets require some political interference; the most practical amount in the world so far was worked out by our founding fathers as they designed our constitution for minimum market interference, but still retaining a monopoly on violence.  No country in the world has ever come close to matching the United States in terms of productivity and standard of living.  Maximum interference with the market, like North Korea's totalitarianism suppresses the free market to the point it barely functions and people become uniformly poor to the point of starvation.<br /><br />Dr. Sandel apparently does not believe in meaningful market function.  By inference, then, he believes sovereignty should pass from the people into government, which flies in the face of virtually every economic study of many hundreds of countries' political economy.  Where the free market is supreme, the quality of life is good.  Where politics overrides the market, we have to stumble over the wreckage caused by government interventions.  As well written and easy to read as his book is, I believe a critically thinking reader might agree that Michael J. Sandel would have been far more comfortable discussing his political economic philosophy with Karl Marx, Mao Tse-tung, and Fidel Castro than he ever would have been with Thomas Jefferson, Friedrich A. Hayek, and Milton Friedman.

0679731369|3.0|Julian Barnes is an English writer who has been shortlisted for the Booker Prize I can't remember how many times (he actually won it, once, with his novel \\"The Sense of an Ending\\").<br /><br />He's a keen Francophile and, apparently, his love of France is a mutual one, with many awards to his name and a title, \\"Commandeur de l'Ordre des Arts et des Letters\\", which I'm sure not many Anglo-Saxons can claim.<br /><br />Barnes wrote several quasi-mainstream novels but, beware, this isn't one. \\"Flaubert's Parrot\\" is in fact a much more complex affair, a literary divertissement testing the boundaries between biography and fiction.<br /><br />On the surface, \\"Flaubert's Parrot\\" is about a retired English doctor obsessed with Gustave Flaubert. This leads him to investigating the mystery surrounding two stuffed parrots, both claiming a role as source of inspiration for one of Flaubert's short stories.<br /><br />Does this little piece of sleuthing eventually lead to an answer? Hardly the point since Barnes is more concerned with non-linear storytelling and subjectivism.<br /><br />Early in the novel you will encounter three different chronologies of Flaubert's life: one is very flattering, the second is starkly negative and the third is made of quotations by the man himself. The life recounted is the same, the resulting story altogether different. Many chapters follow, each either employing a different style or switching narrator.<br /><br />This is a very clever novel and Barnes' talent with words shines throughout its length. Having said this, some of you at this stage might be wondering whether it's an enjoyable one too.<br /><br />I can only answer from personal taste and my reply is, as it often happens, two-fold: \\"yes, but don't expect a page-turner\\".

0553801473|4.0|I went into reading DWD with two things in mind: one, how truly annoyed I was with FFC; and two, how many reviews I'd seen and people I'd heard saying that DWD was as bad or only slightly better than FFC.  As I slogged through the description-laden early parts of the book, I took frequent breaks (something I never did with the first three) when I simply got too tired of hearing about Castle Black politics that involved a bunch of characters I didn't know, or frustrated with Maureen politics that involved a bunch of characters I didn't know with tricky to remember names.  Sometimes I would get so pissed with a particularly lengthy meal or wardrobe description that I'd throw down my book and go do something else.  Basically, I felt the same way I did with FFC: he's spending too much time on needless description, and even the plot advancements don't involve storylines or characters I care about.<br /><br />However, about halfway through the book, a funny thing happened: I started to actually CARE about some of these new characters and plotlines.  The goings-on in Dorne and even places like Volantis began to intrigue me, as did the new directions of old characters like Tyrion (although I won't deny that I never much got into Jon's wildling-saving travails).  It took a book and half to happen, but by the end of DWD, GRRM managed to more or less pull off something pretty difficult in a series: he mostly replaced the plotlines he'd developed in the first three books with new ones.  This is hard to do for the same reason it's hard to make a roller-coaster take a right angle turn; you have to slow it down a lot first.  It took me a long time to let go of the momentum that had built up in SOS, but it did finally happen, and by the end of DWD I was really enthralled with the climax, especially all the forces converging on Maureen in a sequence of chapters that reminded me a little of the excellent sequence at the end of COK during the Battle of the Blackwater.  I think those segments of palpable strife and foreboding surrounding one location, all told from many different perspectives, are the best parts of the series, and the fact that this book had one of those segments automatically earns it 4 stars from me.<br /><br />Yes, I will admit that there is too much description in the book, and the whole thing could have been chopped down or sped up a lot and benefitted greatly from it.  But, on the other hand, I'd rather that it move too slow than too fast.  Another one of this series' strongest points is its way of moving the plot forward significantly without the reader even realizing it.  This is all done through character development and, often, tons of description.  The Red Wedding was great, but how much less powerful would it have been without the reader's knowledge of Walder Frey's demeanor, or Stark characters' many little concessions to honor, or Tywin and, later, Roose's prolonged brooding in Harrenhal?  The red wedding was actually strategized and laid out at the Twins, Harrenhal, and Kings Landing over a long period, but the reader never sees that.  And yet what they DO see hits them like a hammer, because all of the little pieces are there that make it totally reasonable, even if it happened suddenly.<br /><br />The reason I bring up the Red Wedding is that I think a lot of the groundwork for later Red Wedding-esque moments was laid in this book.  The pieces may be taking a little longer (OK, too long) to fall into place in this second batch of plots (and I do think that's what you should consider everything after SOS to be), but that is somewhat understandable when one considers the relatively more mammoth scale of everything post-SOS.  I am confident GRRM will bring the series in for a landing.  That line from the last Dany chapter about \\"going backwards to go forwards\\" could be this series' mantra; because GRRM isn't afraid to go backwards plot-wise by leading his characters on circuitous paths through the story, often skirting the main issues, the plot's forward-jumps resonate with more force.

145162445X|4.0|A Universe from Nothing by Lawrence Krauss<br /><br />Nothing comes from nothing, nothing ever could, right?  There's no such thing as a free lunch?  Apparently not.  As Krauss explains, not only does the something of stars and dust and planets and galaxy proceed naturally from from the \\"nothing\\" of empty space, but the \\"nothing\\" of empty space (which is actually measurably something, since it can be warped and twisted and imposes laws upon that which exists inside it) proceeds from actual nothingness, according to recent models.  The book builds to this point; the rest of it sets the context both socially and cosmologically, explaining both the importance of producing something from nothing and exactly what space and nothing are, and something of the scientific history that led to these models.  The fact that Dawkins wrote the afterward to the book may indicate the social setting.<br /><br />What's Good: Krauss tackles what should be a very complex subject conversationally.  That's not to say everything made perfect sense on the first read-through, or that I was able to get through every page without my eyes losing focus and floating away, but generally the concepts, the explanations, and the descriptions are all comprehensible; I didn't feel like I was ever reading another language.<br /><br />What's Bad: The book was presented with the tone of absolute truth, and I'm not in a position to judge otherwise.  Sometimes the book backs it up - it discusses how the predictions of quantum mechanics have been tested to an accuracy of billionths. But many cutting edge physics theories are discarded a few years later when better tests are run (Krauss discusses this), and I didn't feel that the hardest science and the speculative science was clearly delineated.<br /><br />The Takeaway: Whether the entire book was \\"hard\\" or \\"speculative\\", Krauss has done one thing, at least - he's demonstrated that something did come from \\"nothing\\", and something -could- have come from true nothingness, and that's worth talking about.othingness, and that's worth talking about.

I was also confused at times of the story. I felt like the pacing was off and I was rushed in some spots and in others, the story drug on too long.<br /><br />Overall, I was bored by the plot and the characters and while the writing was better, this novel didn't hold a candle to the 50 Shades of Grey books. I felt like I had to compare them because Bared to You because everywhere I went, I saw the two books together. 0547260695|3.0|The pop-up art in this edition is fantastic. On some pages it is static while other pages are movable via tabs. Still others pop out in the more traditional way when the page is opened. The variety of the way the pop up art is \\"paper engineered\\" adds to the childlike wonder when viewing the world that is at the heart of this classic tale. It is almost as if the Little Prince himself designed them!<br />So why must I give this book less than 5 stars? The new Howard translation manages to take the poetic wonder of the original and reduce it to a \\"grown up\\" version devoid of much of the magic in Katherine Woods' translation. Not having the proficiency needed to read it in the original language I can not be certain if what I loved as a child and learn from again and again as I grow older is there in the words that Antoine de Saint-Exupery penned. I strongly suspect that it is, as the story has been loved by people from almost countless cultures reading in numerous languages. The Woods version, published as the official English translation for the first 55 years or so after Saint-Exupery completed the French original, certainly conveys a poetic sense of wonder that is lacking in the newer Howard account. When I ordered this edition several months ago the description stated the text was the full, unedited translation by Woods. It appears Amazon has since corrected this oversight. I'm sure I was not the only person disappointed when the book arrived to discover the text was Howard's instead of Woods' translation.<br />If you've read the Woods translation already I can recommend this edition on the strength of the pop up art. If you have not read The Little Prince I would recommend you find a used copy of the Woods translation or look for it online.

B008GX2YQQ|5.0|The pressure has well and truly been ramped up in anticipation of this latest Animal Collective release following as it does the commercial breakthrough album 'Merriweather Post Pavilion' and the equally impressive 'Fall Be Kind' EP. The expectations are different this time out, the last album was considered more accessible and even contained a genuine indie hit in the form of 'My Girls' - it goes without saying that many listeners will be hoping 'Centipede Hz' follows a similar path to its predecessor. After living with this album for a week I'm not convinced everyone is going to be won over by the direction the band have taken, this is abrasive, dense, busy - some will baulk at the seemingly cluttered and slippery arrangements. The closest recent parallel for me would be someone purchasing 'Embryonic' by the Flaming Lips hoping to find another 'Yoshimi', both albums are clearly the work of the same band but where the earlier album applies a smooth and clean approach to the production and keeps the writing concise the later release revels in throwing up an alien sound palette of initially perplexing and jarring noise which it marries to far looser song structures. It goes a long way to explaining why I awarded this album 5 stars when you consider I actually rated 'Embryonic' my favourite album of 2009 ahead of 'MPP'!<br /><br />'Centipede Hz' really is a full on sensory assault right from the off - after a quick snippet of radio noise 'Moonjock' jumps straight in with pounding drums and distorted vocals, as a standalone song perhaps not the band's most focused composition but it works perfectly as an extended intro here particularly because it is followed by the incredibly tight tribal meets carnival intensity of 'Today's Supernatural'. I suppose it is at this point I should point out that this album is somewhat dominated by Avey Tare vocally which is fine by me, I've always favoured his style but Lennox fans may be slightly disappointed by the lack of Panda lead vocals. This leads us neatly to track 3, a short number sung by Noah which to these ears has a touch of early Talking Heads to the guitar parts - it is a pleasant diversion but ever so slightly insubstantial.<br /><br />Luckily the album gets back on track with the simply phenomenal 'Apple Sauce' that has just got to be considered one of the band's best ever moments - the composition contains so many separate parts it is unbelievable! The chorus when it arrives for the first time (at the 1:50 mark no less!) is just so perfect for the band melodically and in particular listen out for the added detail provided by the backing vocals which take it to that extra level. Even better, just when you think they may be losing their way and milking the chorus a little too much an outro brilliantly bursts in - 'One, the eagle!!... two, the nobel!!...'. Great stuff.<br /><br />'Wide Eyed' is up next and is a bit of an Animal Collective oddity as it is sung by the returning guitarist Deakin (he sat out MPP) and it does a good job of keeping the momentum going, sounding refreshingly different to all that has preceded it. In a way it is a shame the band haven't incorporated his lead vocals in any of their past material as the only gripe I have is it does have the feel of a guest spot, especially as the rest of the band don't provide any backing vocals - perhaps this won't be a one off going forward? 'Father Time' is a classic Collective composition, it sounds vaguely tropical and is another highlight. I'm always surprised no one ever compares Portner's vocals to Perry Farrell of Jane's Addiction, there are some huge similarities in tone and inflection there - a good thing in my book!<br /><br />'New Town Burnout' is a decent Panda Bear number that builds slowly and comes into its own as the layers of samples build, by the end it has created an intoxicating atmosphere and allowed for a little respite from the full on assault of beats and harsh effects found elsewhere...although this respite proves to be a short lived as a repeated sample of machine noise heralds the start of 'Monkey Rich', a singalong Avey number with a tribal meets industrial feel and chanting vocals in the chorus - one of the more instantly catchy songs and likely to be a live favourite. 'Mercury Man' has a creepy edge with disembodied vocals somewhat rescuing the track at the end, 'Pulleys' is more satisfying and similar to 'Father Time' in being a more typical Animal Collective tune. The disc is rounded off by the excellent and suitably grand 'Amanita' which adds a touch of Eastern flair to the mix, the song gets increasingly psychedelic and distorted as it races to its conclusion.<br /><br />I can see why this album might get a bashing from some quarters and I can also agree that 'Centipede Hz' may not be the certified improvement/refinement of 'Merriweather Post Pavilion' we perhaps expected but as a fan of Animal Collective at their most intense and discordant and as someone who really enjoyed the challenging 'Strawberry Jam' and similarly no holds barred releases from other artists ('Embryonic' and Sufjan Steven's 'Age of Adz' spring to mind) I have to applaud the band for releasing such a uniquely different recording once again - as always essential.

By about the mid-point of the book, I started to lose interest.  The characters lost their depth, and even the supposed plot twists became predictable.  In the end, I felt that it was an interesting story that got away from the author.  Here are some additional specifics...<br /><br />CHARACTERS<br />Pros:<br />The characters are generally complex and real (at least early on).  They have strengths and faults, and, even though they are creatures, their behaviors are contextualized in ways that make you feel as though you understand these people/beings.  I liked Diana a lot in the early pages.  Although I don't think she presents as a thirty-something academic very well, she seems like a real person.  The vampires are more a mixed bag with respect to their depth, but generally I liked the way the author conveyed them in the gray area between right and wrong.<br /><br />Cons:<br />The characters, good and bad, became more unidimensional as the story went on. The bad guys became just bad and the good guys became (mostly) just good.<br /><br />Some characters seemed to be introduced as an afterthought because some aspect of the story were not falling into place properly.  This made the story meander more than it needed to.<br /><br />THE LOVE STORY<br />Pros:<br />Although I rarely admit it, I can get engulfed in a good love story.  In this case, it is a classic forbidden love but with a supernatural twist.  As it started to unfold, I enjoyed the tension of not knowing whether I wanted it to be successful or not.<br /><br />Cons:<br />The emergence of the love story was too obvious too early.<br /><br />The main characters become particularly nauseating as their relationship develops.  My guess is that the author was trying to capture the ferocity of love that comes with finding the right person (something everyone should be lucky enough to experience), but it comes off as more and more childish over time.<br /><br />By the end of the book, the Bishop house was my favorite character.<br /><br />THE INTERTWINING OF WORLDS<br />Pros:<br />The intertwining of the occult world and the modern world was fascinating.  The presence of creatures in every day life was handled with a lot of finesse that made suspension of disbelief very easy.  I even felt myself looking around the world and imagining that we, as humans, might actually be living in such a world.  I really enjoy books that can do that.<br /><br />Cons:<br />The collision of worlds became too predictable, and the human side of things all but disappeared.  As a result, I stopped feeling that sense of being able to feel a part of the story world and felt like an outsider reading about a world that I could not engage.  This was probably the biggest disappointment in a book so long.  By the last 200 pages, I felt like I was reading just to get it over with.<br /><br />Finally, I think Harkness is a talented storyteller and writes very appealing prose.  I just think this book needed some serious and strong editing to make it hang together.  Now, I will have to decide if the strengths are enough to get me to try the sequel.

B000XCZGV8|3.0|When as a child growing up in the Bay Area he was told by his father that the notorious serial killer the Zodiac had announced he was targeting children on school buses, David Fincher says he discovered the ultimate boogeyman for his childhood. His haunting by the case inspired his first movie ever about serial killers, the beautiful and memorably cruel SE7EN, and then later resulted in this extremely elaborate recreation of the Zodiac's murders and the attempts by three men (a cartoonist for the San Francisco Chronicle, his reporter colleague, and a local detective) in solving the killer's identity (which to this day remains officially unsolved, though the film makes clear who the three men think committed the murders). By focusing on the puzzle-solvers rather than on the committing of the crimes themselves (which are disposed of nearly completely in the film's first half) or the victims, Fincher is truer to his interests but makes a duller and less engaging movie than he might have otherwise. He seems to care very little about the Zodiac's victims or their families or the survivors of his crimes. The murders are re-staged with painstaking scrupulousness to detail, but except for a lovely beginning sequence of two of the most famous of the Zodiac's victims meeting to drive to a lover's lane amidst a trippy Independence Day evening celebration in a local neighborhood, there's no sense of the victims really as people at all. The stakes of the film are thus not very high: all you care about in the end is seeing the puzzle solved for its own sake.<br /><br />Exacerbating the problem is the thinness of the three central protagonists. Although Jake Gyllenhaal brings his beautiful haunted eyes to the role of the cartoonist, he doesn't really flesh out the part beyond the descriptors of \\"obsessed\\" and \\"former Eagle Scout.\\" Robert Downey Jr. relies on tricks he uses far too often playing yet another loveable mouthy screw-up with substance abuse problems; only Mark Ruffalo,as his darkest and most intense, seems to generate much heat as the grouchy detective. There are beautiful sequences in the film, mostly at what would be its most difficult junctures, i.e. to show the considerable passing of time: one involves a beautiful CGI re-creation of the building of the Transamerica Pyramid (via simulated time-lapse photography). Fincher is marvelous as solving technical problems but fails to solve the larger problem of making the story involving for audiences not as obsessed with the Zodiac case as he himself already is. The film is much longer than it needed to be, and much less exciting. The two-disc director's cut is worth seeing for two excellent appended documentaries, one about the case against Arthur Leigh Allen, the prime suspect in the case, and the other (of particular beauty and elegance) about the four certain murderous attacks of the Zodiac killer, which involves interviews with the detectives and policemen originally on the cases and the two survivors of the attacks.

0307951529|3.0|First, I want to say that I enjoyed this book a great deal. I finished it instead of losing interest halfway through, so that says a lot about Chris as a writer. He made the topic enjoyable and exciting.<br /><br />That said, when I finished $100 Startup, I felt like I'd missed something. I went back through the notes that I'd jotted down throughout the pages, and over and over again I'd written the word HOW?? with a line underneath it, signifying that the author hadn't given enough information. How exactly did business owner A accomplish task B? How exactly did person X find their way out of situation Y? There were a lot of places where there needed to be more information, especially if the point of the book is to inform potential entrepreneurs that might face a similar situation as was being described.<br /><br />I was also left feeling like there should have been more details about a few of the businesses Chris discusses, as I found myself more than once putting the book down and looking up the name he mentions to find out exactly what he was talking about. It was a bit of a distraction that could have been avoided by giving an additional sentence where he vaguely mentions a person's business *type*, but doesn't explain exactly what it is they do.<br /><br />Towards the end, I felt like the book ran out of steam. The chapter on franchising yourself felt disjointed, like it was a conglomerate of a few other half-written chapters that didn't quite come together in the final edit. That could be the editor's issue, but still, the book was affected. The last chapter didn't really accomplish the task of pulling everything together; rather, it limply dangled on the end as if asking, \\"What are you going to do with all that you've just read?\\" Where's the excitement building? The GET OUT THERE AND GET TO IT feeling? Chris had done such a good job of this throughout the book, but the end wasn't terribly inspiring.<br /><br />The final thing that bummed me out is that there were a few areas of the book where I wanted more information, and when I went to the 100startup.com website, there wasn't much there. I was funneled into Chris' site, where I learned he's more than happy to sell you one of his products that contains all the stuff that didn't end up in the book for hundreds of dollars. (yes, really - like $150-over $400. Yikes!) I was surprised to see that he'd reserved some of the most informative stuff for his own private products - something I'm surprised his editors were ok with. Like, how exactly does one set the value on their products? The book doesn't say - it just recommends business owners set a price based on the product's value, not what it cost you to make it. Ok, well, where's the information that tells you how to gauge value? It's sort of ironic that I'd have to pay a couple hundred bucks to find out more about what to price my own products at.<br /><br />So, read the first 3/4 this book because it's a fun read and you'll get some good details, but if you want to go the distance with Chris, plan on shelling out a few hundred bucks.were a few areas of the book where I wanted more information, and when I went to the 100startup.com website, there wasn't much there. B005LAIIRQ|4.0|`The Grey' isn't usually my kind of movie, but after hearing nothing but good things about it, I gave it a watch and I wasn't disappointed. It's actually quite a suspenseful survival-thriller and I loved it from the first minute to when the credits started rolling.<br /><br />Liam Neeson is John Ottway, a hunter working on an Alaskan drilling station who keeps the wolves away with his trusty rifle. On his flight home, his plane crashes in the middle of nowhere with just him and a handful of survivors, as they try and make their way to people, they find themselves under siege by wolves while battling it out with the cold weather and injuries.<br /><br />Director Joe Carnahan knows how to keep things edgy, he uses the thick darkness of night and the low visibility of the blizzard to great effect; in one scene, the wolves let out a terrifying howl in response to Ottway's challenge and all we can see are their cold breaths and we never know when one, or several, are going to jump out and maul one of the survivor's to death. And it helps that they also give the wolves an almost eerily human quality; they take revenge for the fallen, they send pack outcasts to test the group, they use tactics...you get the feeling that Ottway and co. aren't just facing a pack of wild, mongrel wolves, they're facing an organized and formidable enemy. The dangers don't just come from the wolves, the weather, the injuries and the dangerous terrain the group traverse only serve to make their situation that much more dire.<br /><br />But it's not all action and I'm thankful that Carnahan also had the mind to take time out to build the characters, give us insight into their pasts and their relationships with each other and to get you emotionally connected to them, especially John Ottway. It really made their deaths that much more painful to watch in the end, especially further into the movie, where the remaining guys had already been through so much, a few of the deaths might even make you a little misty. Along the way you get a little exploration of death, faith, God and courage to give you a little food for thought, necessary or not, it does give the film some appreciated depth. `The Grey' is worth the watch.

0374203032|2.0|I first read a review of this book in The Economist.  It noted \\"Anyone who sets store by capitalism and markets will find [this book] uncomfortable reading. [It] should be read all the same.\\"  Ok, I'm one of those kind of people and like to be challenged.  So far so good.  Unfortunately, the author lost me in the introduction.  He attributes the financial crisis of 2008 as a market failure and takes off from there.  At the end of the introduction, he says:  \\"When we think of the morality of the markets, we think first of Wall Street banks and their reckless misdeeds....\\"  No mention of all of the market distortions from the artificial low interest rates imposed by the Federal Reserve to the almost monopolistic actions of the 2 big GSEs:  Fannie & Freddie, to various incentives by the US tax code & other regulations to over-borrow for housing to the mysterious capital requirements discrepancies imposed first by the SEC and later Basel II to finally the massive bailouts of financial institutions all over the world that confirmed to big institutions that lending prudence is not really necessary.  OK, lots of folks are unwilling to acknowledge these market distortions and instead focus on a small piece of \\"deregulation\\" in the form of the Gramm-Leach-Billey repeal of Glass-Steagall.  I'll forgive the author on that one but I'll remain skeptical.<br /><br />From that foundation, the author launches into a series of anecdotes that are intended to show that markets can produce \\"immoral\\" outcomes or something.  In the first chapter, he rails on how terrible it is when people pay other people to stand in line for them without acknowledging the market distortions that create those lines in the first place. (That said, to the author's credit, he does a reasonably thorough job of explaining the different dynamics and perspectives on this topic.)  In the next chapter, he rails on how terrible it is that people are offered cash to get sterilized, to get good grades,  to lose weight, etc. without acknowledging the market distortions of government dictated health insurance, government run schools, etc.  Then he includes a strange chapter on hiring friends, paying for apologies, why people resist giving cash gifts, etc.  The next chapter focuses on the \\"horrors\\" of the life settlement industry where people with terminal diseases ell their life insurance policies to strangers for upfront cash so that they can enjoy their final days without acknowledging that the life insurance industry's primary foundation is it's favorable tax treatment.  In the final chapter, he complains about how money has infiltrated into organized sports (and other public activities) in terms naming rights, luxury skyboxes, and other kinds of advertising.  He seems to miss his youth when baseball tickets were $1.50 and owners exploited their federal exemption from antitrust laws to underpay their players.<br /><br />I ended up skimming the book in an evening.  There were a few slightly interesting anecdotes but overall this felt like a bunch of personal complaints about economic activities that irritate the author.  Does my bias shine through?
